   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Association football
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>

      "Soccer" redirects here. For other uses, see

     <div class="hatnote">
      This article is about the sport of association football.  For football associations, see
      <a href="/wiki/Football_association" title="Football association">
       Football association
      </a>
      .  For the English association, see
      <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">
       The Football Association
      </a>
      .
     </div>

     <p>
      <b>
       Association football
      </b>
      , more commonly known as
      <b>
       football
      </b>
      or
      <b>
       soccer
      </b>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Team_sport" title="Team sport">
       sport
      </a>
      played between two teams of eleven
      <a href="/wiki/Football_player" title="Football player">
       players
      </a>
      with a spherical
      <a href="/wiki/Ball_(association_football)" title="Ball (association football)">
       ball
      </a>
      . It is played by 250 million players in over 200 countries, making it the world's most popular sport.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EB-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The game is played on a rectangular
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_football_pitch" title="Association football pitch">
       field
      </a>
      with a
      <a href="/wiki/Goal_(sport)" title="Goal (sport)">
       goal
      </a>
      at each end. The object of the game is to score by getting the ball into the opposing goal.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Goalkeeper_(association_football)" title="Goalkeeper (association football)">
       goalkeepers
      </a>
      are the only players allowed to touch the ball with their hands or arms while it is in play and then only in their
      <a href="/wiki/Penalty_area" title="Penalty area">
       penalty area
      </a>
      . Outfield players mostly use their feet to strike or pass the ball, but may use their head or torso to strike the ball instead. The team that scores the most goals by the end of the match wins. If the score is level at the end of the game, either a
      <a href="/wiki/Tie_(draw)" title="Tie (draw)">
       draw
      </a>
      is declared or the game goes into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_time" title="Extra time">
       extra time
      </a>
      and/or a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penalty_shootout_(association_football)" title="Penalty shootout (association football)">
       penalty shootout
      </a>
      depending on the format of the competition. The
      <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_the_Game_(association_football)" title="Laws of the Game (association football)">
       Laws of the Game
      </a>
      were originally codified in England by
      <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">
       The Football Association
      </a>
      in 1863. Association football is governed internationally by
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">
       FIFA
      </a>
      , who organizes the quadrennial
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">
       FIFA World Cup
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">
       Women's World Cup
      </a>
      tournaments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">
        <a href="#See_also">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          9
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          See also
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">
        <a href="#References">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          10
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          References
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20">
        <a href="#External_links">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          11
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          External links
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_association_football" title="History of association football">
       History of association football
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greek_Football_Player.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="229" data-file-width="215" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Ancient_Greek_Football_Player.jpg" width="215"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greek_Football_Player.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
         Ancient Greek
        </a>
        episkyros player on an
        <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">
         Attic
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Lekythos" title="Lekythos">
         lekythos
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="995" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg/220px-One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg/330px-One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg/440px-One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:One_Hundred_Children_in_the_Long_Spring.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Children playing
        <a href="/wiki/Cuju" title="Cuju">
         cuju
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">
         Song dynasty
        </a>
        China
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The earliest known football-type game in Europe was Phaininda, an
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
       ancient Greek
      </a>
      game that was first recorded in the fourth century BCE by
      <a href="/wiki/Antiphanes_(comic_poet)" title="Antiphanes (comic poet)">
       Antiphanes (c.408 to 334 BCE)
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-home.lu.lv_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-home.lu.lv-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Phaininda was similar to the Greek game
      <a href="/wiki/Episkyros" title="Episkyros">
       Episkyros
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa.com_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fifa.com-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An image of an Episkyros player taken from an ancient stone carving held by the
      <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_Museum" title="Acropolis Museum">
       Acropolis Museum
      </a>
      appears on the
      <a href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Championship" title="UEFA European Championship">
       UEFA European Championship
      </a>
      Cup.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus" title="Athenaeus">
       Athenaeus
      </a>
      , writing in 228 AD, stated the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
       Roman
      </a>
      ball game
      <a href="/wiki/Harpastum" title="Harpastum">
       Harpastum
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa.com_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fifa.com-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      was a version of Phaininda.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-home.lu.lv_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-home.lu.lv-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As with pre-codified "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mob_Football" title="Mob Football">
       Mob Football
      </a>
      ", the antecedent of all
      <a href="/wiki/Football" title="Football">
       modern football codes
      </a>
      , these three games involved more handling the ball than kicking.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">
       FIFA
      </a>
      the competitive game
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cuju" title="Cuju">
        cuju
       </a>
      </i>
      is the earliest form of football for which there is scientific evidence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa-or_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fifa-or-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Documented evidence of an activity resembling football can be found in the Chinese
      <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">
       military
      </a>
      manual
      <a href="/wiki/Zhan_Guo_Ce" title="Zhan Guo Ce">
       Zhan Guo Ce
      </a>
      compiled between the third and second century BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa-or_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fifa-or-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Players were tasked with kicking a ball into a net affixed to bamboo poles, using only their feet, chest, back or shoulders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Non-competitive games included
      <a href="/wiki/Kemari" title="Kemari">
       kemari
      </a>
      in Japan,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chuk-guk" title="Chuk-guk">
       chuk-guk
      </a>
      in Korea and
      <a href="/wiki/Woggabaliri" title="Woggabaliri">
       woggabaliri
      </a>
      in Australia.
     </p>
     <p>
      Association football in itself does not have a classical history.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Notwithstanding any similarities to other ball games played around the world FIFA have recognised that no historical connection exists with any game played in antiquity outside Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The modern rules of association football are based on the mid-19th century efforts to standardise the widely
      <a href="/wiki/English_public_school_football_games" title="English public school football games">
       varying forms of football
      </a>
      played in the
      <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">
       public schools
      </a>
      of England. The history of football in England dates back to
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_football" title="Medieval football">
       at least the eighth century AD
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_Rules" title="Cambridge Rules">
       Cambridge Rules
      </a>
      , first drawn up at Cambridge University in 1848, were particularly influential in the development of subsequent codes, including association football. The Cambridge Rules were written at
      <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">
       Trinity College, Cambridge
      </a>
      , at a meeting attended by representatives from
      <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">
       Eton
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Harrow_School" title="Harrow School">
       Harrow
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rugby_School" title="Rugby School">
       Rugby
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Winchester_College" title="Winchester College">
       Winchester
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_School" title="Shrewsbury School">
       Shrewsbury
      </a>
      schools. They were not universally adopted. During the 1850s, many clubs unconnected to schools or universities were formed throughout the English-speaking world, to play various forms of football. Some came up with their own distinct codes of rules, most notably the
      <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_F.C." title="Sheffield F.C.">
       Sheffield Football Club
      </a>
      , formed by former public school pupils in 1857,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which led to formation of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheffield_%26_Hallamshire_Football_Association" title="Sheffield &amp; Hallamshire Football Association">
       Sheffield FA
      </a>
      in 1867. In 1862,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._C._Thring" title="J. C. Thring">
       John Charles Thring
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Uppingham_School" title="Uppingham School">
       Uppingham School
      </a>
      also devised an influential set of rules.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      These ongoing efforts contributed to the formation of
      <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">
       The Football Association
      </a>
      (The FA) in 1863, which first met on the morning of 26 October 1863 at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemasons%27_Tavern" title="Freemasons' Tavern">
       Freemasons' Tavern
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Queen_Street" title="Great Queen Street">
       Great Queen Street
      </a>
      , London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAhistory_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FAhistory-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The only school to be represented on this occasion was
      <a href="/wiki/Charterhouse_School" title="Charterhouse School">
       Charterhouse
      </a>
      . The Freemason's Tavern was the setting for five more meetings between October and December, which eventually produced the first comprehensive set of rules. At the final meeting, the first FA treasurer, the representative from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackheath_Rugby_Club" title="Blackheath Rugby Club">
       Blackheath
      </a>
      , withdrew his club from the FA over the removal of two draft rules at the previous meeting: the first allowed for running with the ball in hand; the second for obstructing such a run by hacking (kicking an opponent in the shins), tripping and holding. Other
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_rugby_union" title="History of rugby union">
       English rugby clubs followed this lead
      </a>
      and did not join the FA and instead in 1871 formed the
      <a href="/wiki/Rugby_Football_Union" title="Rugby Football Union">
       Rugby Football Union
      </a>
      . The eleven remaining clubs, under the charge of
      <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Cobb_Morley" title="Ebenezer Cobb Morley">
       Ebenezer Cobb Morley
      </a>
      , went on to ratify the original thirteen laws of the game.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAhistory_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FAhistory-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These rules included handling of the ball by "marks" and the lack of a crossbar, rules which made it remarkably similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_rules_football" title="Australian rules football">
       Victorian rules football
      </a>
      being developed at that time in Australia. The Sheffield FA played by its own rules until the 1870s with the FA absorbing some of its rules until there was little difference between the games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merger_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Merger-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1stRoyalEngineers.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="659" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/1stRoyalEngineers.png/220px-1stRoyalEngineers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/1stRoyalEngineers.png/330px-1stRoyalEngineers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/1stRoyalEngineers.png/440px-1stRoyalEngineers.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1stRoyalEngineers.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Royal_Engineers_A.F.C." title="Royal Engineers A.F.C.">
         Royal Engineers
        </a>
        team who reached the first
        <a href="/wiki/1872_FA_Cup_Final" title="1872 FA Cup Final">
         FA Cup final
        </a>
        in 1872
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The world's oldest football competition is the
      <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">
       FA Cup
      </a>
      , which was founded by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C._W._Alcock" title="C. W. Alcock">
       C. W. Alcock
      </a>
      and has been contested by English teams since 1872. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scotland_v_England_(1872)" title="Scotland v England (1872)">
       first official international football match
      </a>
      also took place in 1872, between Scotland and England in
      <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">
       Glasgow
      </a>
      , again at the instigation of C. W. Alcock. England is also home to the world's first
      <a href="/wiki/The_Football_League" title="The Football League">
       football league
      </a>
      , which was founded in
      <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">
       Birmingham
      </a>
      in 1888 by
      <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">
       Aston Villa
      </a>
      director
      <a href="/wiki/William_McGregor_(football)" title="William McGregor (football)">
       William McGregor
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The original format contained 12 clubs from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Midlands" title="English Midlands">
       Midlands
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">
       Northern England
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The laws of the game are determined by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Football_Association_Board" title="International Football Association Board">
       International Football Association Board
      </a>
      (IFAB).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Board was formed in 1886
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      after a meeting in
      <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">
       Manchester
      </a>
      of The Football Association, the
      <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Football_Association" title="Scottish Football Association">
       Scottish Football Association
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Football_Association_of_Wales" title="Football Association of Wales">
       Football Association of Wales
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Irish_Football_Association" title="Irish Football Association">
       Irish Football Association
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">
       FIFA
      </a>
      , the international football body, was formed in Paris in 1904 and declared that they would adhere to Laws of the Game of the Football Association.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wherebegan_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wherebegan-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The growing popularity of the international game led to the admittance of FIFA representatives to the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Football_Association_Board" title="International Football Association Board">
       International Football Association Board
      </a>
      in 1913. The board consists of four representatives from FIFA and one representative from each of the four British associations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFAB_works_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IFAB_works-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Today, football is played at a professional level all over the world. Millions of people regularly go to football stadiums to follow their favourite teams,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while billions more watch the game on television or on the internet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A very large number of people also play football at an amateur level. According to a survey conducted by FIFA published in 2001, over 240 million people from more than 200 countries regularly play football.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Football has the highest global television audience in sport.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In many parts of the world football evokes great passions and plays an important role in the life of individual
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fan_(aficionado)" title="Fan (aficionado)">
       fans
      </a>
      , local communities, and even nations. R. Kapuscinski says that Europeans who are polite, modest, or humble fall easily into rage when playing or watching football games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soccer_war_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-soccer_war-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire_national_football_team" title="Cte d'Ivoire national football team">
       Cte d'Ivoire national football team
      </a>
      helped secure a truce to the nation's
      <a href="/wiki/First_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="First Ivorian Civil War">
       civil war
      </a>
      in 2006
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it helped further reduce tensions between government and rebel forces in 2007 by playing a match in the rebel capital of
      <a href="/wiki/Bouak%C3%A9" title="Bouak">
       Bouak
      </a>
      , an occasion that brought both armies together peacefully for the first time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By contrast, football is widely considered to have been the final proximate cause for the
      <a href="/wiki/Football_War" title="Football War">
       Football War
      </a>
      in June 1969 between
      <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">
       El Salvador
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">
       Honduras
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sport also exacerbated tensions at the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars" title="Yugoslav Wars">
       Yugoslav Wars
      </a>
      of the 1990s, when a match between
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NK_Dinamo_Zagreb" title="NK Dinamo Zagreb">
       Dinamo Zagreb
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Star_Belgrade" title="Red Star Belgrade">
       Red Star Belgrade
      </a>
      degenerated into rioting in May 1990.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Women.27s_association_football">
       Women's association football
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_association_football" title="Women's association football">
       Women's association football
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mia1997.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="597" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Mia1997.JPG/220px-Mia1997.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Mia1997.JPG/330px-Mia1997.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Mia1997.JPG/440px-Mia1997.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mia1997.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A women's international match between the United States and Germany
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Association football has been played by women since at least the time of the first recorded women's games in the late 19th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCwomen-Gregory_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBCwomen-Gregory-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has traditionally been associated with charity games and physical exercise, particularly in the United Kingdom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCwomen-Gregory_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBCwomen-Gregory-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the late 1960s and early 1970s women's association football was organised in the United Kingdom, eventually becoming the most prominent
      <a href="/wiki/Team_sport" title="Team sport">
       team sport
      </a>
      for British women.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCwomen-Gregory_39-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBCwomen-Gregory-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The growth in women's football has seen major competitions being launched at both
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women%27s_football_around_the_world" title="Women's football around the world">
       national
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_competitions_in_women%27s_football" title="International competitions in women's football">
       international
      </a>
      level mirroring the male competitions. Women's football has faced many struggles. It had a "golden age" in the United Kingdom in the early 1920s when crowds reached 50,000 at some matches;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      this was stopped on 5 December 1921 when England's Football Association voted to ban the game from grounds used by its member clubs. The FA's ban was rescinded in December 1969 with UEFA voting to officially recognise women's football in 1971.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCwomen-Gregory_39-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBCwomen-Gregory-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">
       FIFA Women's World Cup
      </a>
      was inaugurated in 1991 and has been held every four years since,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while women's football has been an Olympic event
      <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_1996_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 1996 Summer Olympics  Women's tournament">
       since 1996
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_names">
       Etymology and names
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Names_for_association_football" title="Names for association football">
       Names for association football
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The rules of association football were codified in England by the Football Association in 1863 and the name
      <i>
       association football
      </i>
      was coined to distinguish the game from the
      <a href="/wiki/Football" title="Football">
       other forms of football
      </a>
      played at the time, specifically
      <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">
       rugby football
      </a>
      . The term
      <i>
       soccer
      </i>
      originated in England, first appearing in the 1880s as an
      <a href="/wiki/Oxford_%22-er%22" title='Oxford "-er"'>
       Oxford "-er"
      </a>
      abbreviation of the word "association".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Within the
      <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">
       English-speaking world
      </a>
      , association football is now usually called
      <i>
       football
      </i>
      in the United Kingdom and mainly
      <i>
       soccer
      </i>
      in Canada and the United States. People in Australia, Ireland and New Zealand use either or both terms, although national associations in Australia and New Zealand now primarily use "football" for the formal name.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Gameplay">
       Gameplay
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2568" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg/220px-U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg/330px-U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg/440px-U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U20-WorldCup2007-Okotie-Onka_edit2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A goalkeeper saving a close-range shot from inside the penalty area
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Association football is played in accordance with a set of rules known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_the_Game_(association_football)" title="Laws of the Game (association football)">
       Laws of the Game
      </a>
      . The game is played using a spherical ball of 68.569.5cm (27.027.4in) circumference,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      known as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ball_(association_football)" title="Ball (association football)">
        football
       </a>
      </i>
      (or
      <i>
       soccer ball
      </i>
      ). Two teams of eleven players each compete to get the ball into the other team's goal (between the posts and under the bar), thereby scoring a goal. The team that has scored more goals at the end of the game is the winner; if both teams have scored an equal number of goals then the game is a draw. Each team is led by a
      <a href="/wiki/Captain_(association_football)" title="Captain (association football)">
       captain
      </a>
      who has only one official responsibility as mandated by the Laws of the Game: to be involved in the coin toss prior to kick-off or
      <a href="/wiki/Penalty_kick" title="Penalty kick">
       penalty kicks
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laws51-52_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-laws51-52-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The primary law is that players other than
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goalkeeper_(football)" title="Goalkeeper (football)">
       goalkeepers
      </a>
      may not deliberately handle the ball with their hands or arms during play, though they do use their hands during a
      <a href="/wiki/Throw-in" title="Throw-in">
       throw-in
      </a>
      restart. Although players usually use their feet to move the ball around, they may use any part of their body (notably, "heading" with the forehead)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      other than their hands or arms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouls_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fouls-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Within normal play, all players are free to play the ball in any direction and move throughout the pitch, though the ball cannot be received in an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offside_(football)" title="Offside (football)">
       offside
      </a>
      position.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-offsidelaw_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-offsidelaw-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg/220px-20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg/330px-20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg/440px-20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20121209_PSG-Juvisy_-_Kenza_Dali_04.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A player executing a slide
        <a href="/wiki/Tackle_(football_move)" title="Tackle (football move)">
         tackle
        </a>
        to dispossess an opponent
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In game play, players attempt to create goal-scoring opportunities through individual control of the ball, such as by
      <a href="/wiki/Dribbling" title="Dribbling">
       dribbling
      </a>
      , passing the ball to a team-mate, and by taking shots at the goal, which is guarded by the opposing goalkeeper. Opposing players may try to regain control of the ball by intercepting a pass or through
      <a href="/wiki/Tackle_(football_move)#Association_football" title="Tackle (football move)">
       tackling
      </a>
      the opponent in possession of the ball; however, physical contact between opponents is restricted. Football is generally a free-flowing game, with play stopping only when the ball has left the field of play or when play is stopped by the
      <a href="/wiki/Referee_(association_football)" title="Referee (association football)">
       referee
      </a>
      for an infringement of the rules. After a stoppage, play recommences with a specified restart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restart_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-restart-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At a professional level, most matches produce only a few goals. For example, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FA_Premier_League_2005-06" title="FA Premier League 2005-06">
       200506 season
      </a>
      of the English
      <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">
       Premier League
      </a>
      produced an average of 2.48 goals per match.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Laws of the Game do not specify any player positions other than goalkeeper,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAW301_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LAW301-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but a number of
      <a href="/wiki/Association_football_positions" title="Association football positions">
       specialised roles
      </a>
      have evolved. Broadly, these include three main categories:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Striker_(association_football)" title="Striker (association football)">
       strikers
      </a>
      , or forwards, whose main task is to score goals;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defender_(football)" title="Defender (football)">
       defenders
      </a>
      , who specialise in preventing their opponents from scoring; and
      <a href="/wiki/Midfielder" title="Midfielder">
       midfielders
      </a>
      , who dispossess the opposition and keep possession of the ball to pass it to the forwards on their team. Players in these positions are referred to as outfield players, to distinguish them from the goalkeeper. These positions are further subdivided according to the area of the field in which the player spends most time. For example, there are central defenders, and left and right midfielders. The ten outfield players may be arranged in any combination. The number of players in each position determines the style of the team's play; more forwards and fewer defenders creates a more aggressive and offensive-minded game, while the reverse creates a slower, more defensive style of play. While players typically spend most of the game in a specific position, there are few restrictions on player movement, and players can switch positions at any time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The layout of a team's players is known as a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Formation_(association_football)" title="Formation (association football)">
        formation
       </a>
      </i>
      . Defining the team's formation and tactics is usually the prerogative of the team's
      <a href="/wiki/Manager_(association_football)" title="Manager (association football)">
       manager
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">
       Laws
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      "Rules of football" redirects here. For the rules of other football games, see
      <a href="/wiki/Football" title="Football">
       Football
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_the_Game_(association_football)" title="Laws of the Game (association football)">
       Laws of the Game (association football)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are 17 laws in the official Laws of the Game, each containing a collection of stipulation and guidelines. The same laws are designed to apply to all levels of football, although certain modifications for groups such as juniors, seniors, women and people with physical disabilities are permitted. The laws are often framed in broad terms, which allow flexibility in their application depending on the nature of the game. The Laws of the Game are published by FIFA, but are maintained by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Football_Association_Board" title="International Football Association Board">
       International Football Association Board
      </a>
      (IFAB).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition to the seventeen laws, numerous IFAB decisions and other directives contribute to the regulation of football.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Players.2C_equipment.2C_and_officials">
       Players, equipment, and officials
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Association_football_positions" title="Association football positions">
       Association football positions
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Formation_(association_football)" title="Formation (association football)">
       Formation (association football)
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kit_(association_football)" title="Kit (association football)">
       Kit (association football)
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Howard_Webb3.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="538" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Howard_Webb3.jpg/220px-Howard_Webb3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Howard_Webb3.jpg/330px-Howard_Webb3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Howard_Webb3.jpg/440px-Howard_Webb3.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Howard_Webb3.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Referee_(association_football)" title="Referee (association football)">
         referee
        </a>
        officiates in a football match
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Each team consists of a maximum of eleven players (excluding
      <a href="/wiki/Substitute_(association_football)" title="Substitute (association football)">
       substitutes
      </a>
      ), one of whom must be the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goalkeeper_(football)" title="Goalkeeper (football)">
       goalkeeper
      </a>
      . Competition rules may state a minimum number of players required to constitute a team, which is usually seven. Goalkeepers are the only players allowed to play the ball with their hands or arms, provided they do so within the
      <a href="/wiki/Penalty_area" title="Penalty area">
       penalty area
      </a>
      in front of their own goal. Though there are a variety of
      <a href="/wiki/Association_football_positions" title="Association football positions">
       positions
      </a>
      in which the outfield (non-goalkeeper) players are strategically placed by a coach, these positions are not defined or required by the Laws.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAW301_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LAW301-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The basic equipment or
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Kit_(association_football)" title="Kit (association football)">
        kit
       </a>
      </i>
      players are required to wear includes a shirt, shorts, socks, footwear and adequate
      <a href="/wiki/Shin_guard" title="Shin guard">
       shin guards
      </a>
      . An
      <a href="/wiki/Jockstrap" title="Jockstrap">
       athletic supporter
      </a>
      and protective cup is highly recommended for male players by medical experts and professionals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Association_football_headgear" title="Association football headgear">
       Headgear
      </a>
      is not a required piece of basic equipment, but players today may choose to wear it to protect themselves from head injury. Players are forbidden to wear or use anything that is dangerous to themselves or another player, such as jewellery or watches. The goalkeeper must wear clothing that is easily distinguishable from that worn by the other players and the match officials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A number of players may be replaced by substitutes during the course of the game. The maximum number of substitutions permitted in most competitive international and domestic league games is three, though the permitted number may vary in other competitions or in
      <a href="/wiki/Exhibition_game" title="Exhibition game">
       friendly matches
      </a>
      . Common reasons for a substitution include injury, tiredness, ineffectiveness, a tactical switch, or
      <a href="/wiki/Timewasting" title="Timewasting">
       timewasting
      </a>
      at the end of a finely poised game. In standard adult matches, a player who has been substituted may not take further part in a match.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      IFAB recommends "that a match should not continue if there are fewer than seven players in either team." Any decision regarding points awarded for abandoned games is left to the individual football associations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div id="Match_officials">
     </div>
     <p>
      A game is officiated by a
      <a href="/wiki/Referee_(association_football)" title="Referee (association football)">
       referee
      </a>
      , who has "full authority to enforce the Laws of the Game in connection with the match to which he has been appointed" (Law 5), and whose decisions are final. The referee is assisted by two
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assistant_referee" title="Assistant referee">
       assistant referees
      </a>
      . In many high-level games there is also a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_official" title="Fourth official">
       fourth official
      </a>
      who assists the referee and may replace another official should the need arise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ball">
       Ball
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ball_(association_football)" title="Ball (association football)">
       Ball (association football)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The ball is spherical with a circumference of between 68 and 70 centimetres (27 and 28in), a weight in the range of 410 to 450 grams (14 to 16oz), and a pressure of between 0.6 and 1.1 bars (8.5 and 15.6psi) at sea level. In the past the ball was made up of leather panels sewn together, with a latex bladder for pressurisation, but modern balls at all levels of the game are now synthetic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pitch">
       Pitch
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Football_pitch" title="Football pitch">
       Football pitch
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Football_pitch_metric.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Football_pitch_metric.svg/400px-Football_pitch_metric.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Football_pitch_metric.svg/600px-Football_pitch_metric.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Football_pitch_metric.svg/800px-Football_pitch_metric.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Football_pitch_metric.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Standard pitch measurements (
        <a href="/wiki/File:Football_pitch_imperial.svg" title="File:Football pitch imperial.svg">
         See Imperial version
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As the Laws were formulated in England, and were initially administered solely by the four British football associations within
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IFAB" title="IFAB">
       IFAB
      </a>
      , the standard dimensions of a football pitch were originally expressed in
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">
       imperial units
      </a>
      . The Laws now express dimensions with approximate
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">
       metric
      </a>
      equivalents (followed by traditional units in brackets), though popular use tends to continue to use traditional units in English-speaking countries with a relatively recent history of
      <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">
       metrication
      </a>
      (or only partial metrication), such as Britain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The length of the pitch for international adult matches is in the range of 100110m (110120yd) and the width is in the range of 6475m (7080yd). Fields for non-international matches may be 90120m (100130yd) length and 4590m (50100yd) in width, provided that the pitch does not become square. In 2008, the IFAB initially approved a fixed size of 105m (344ft) long and 68m (223ft) wide as a standard pitch dimension for international matches;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      however, this decision was later put on hold and was never actually implemented.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The longer boundary lines are
      <i>
       touchlines
      </i>
      , while the shorter boundaries (on which the goals are placed) are
      <i>
       goal lines
      </i>
      . A rectangular goal is positioned at the middle of each goal line.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The inner edges of the vertical goal posts must be 7.32m (8yd) apart, and the lower edge of the horizontal crossbar supported by the goal posts must be 2.44m (8ft) above the ground. Nets are usually placed behind the goal, but are not required by the Laws.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In front of the goal is the penalty area. This area is marked by the goal line, two lines starting on the goal line 16.5m (18yd) from the goalposts and extending 16.5m (18yd) into the pitch perpendicular to the goal line, and a line joining them. This area has a number of functions, the most prominent being to mark where the goalkeeper may handle the ball and where a penalty foul by a member of the defending team becomes punishable by a penalty kick. Other markings define the position of the ball or players at
      <a href="/wiki/Kick-off_(association_football)" title="Kick-off (association football)">
       kick-offs
      </a>
      , goal kicks, penalty kicks and corner kicks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Duration_and_tie-breaking_methods">
       Duration and tie-breaking methods
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A standard adult football match consists of two periods of 45 minutes each, known as halves. Each half runs continuously, meaning that the clock is not stopped when the ball is out of play. There is usually a 15-minute half-time break between halves. The end of the match is known as full-time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule7.2_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rule7.2-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The referee is the official timekeeper for the match, and may make an allowance for time lost through substitutions, injured players requiring attention, or other stoppages. This added time is called
      <i>
       additional time
      </i>
      in FIFA documents,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but is most commonly referred to as
      <i>
       stoppage time
      </i>
      or
      <i>
       injury time
      </i>
      , while
      <i>
       loss time
      </i>
      can also be used as a synonym. The duration of stoppage time is at the sole discretion of the referee. The referee alone signals the end of the match. In matches where a fourth official is appointed, toward the end of the half the referee signals how many minutes of stoppage time he intends to add. The fourth official then informs the players and spectators by holding up a board showing this number. The signalled stoppage time may be further extended by the referee.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule7.2_69-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-rule7.2-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Added time was introduced because of an incident which happened in 1891 during a match between
      <a href="/wiki/Stoke_City_F.C." title="Stoke City F.C.">
       Stoke
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">
       Aston Villa
      </a>
      . Trailing 10 and with just two minutes remaining, Stoke were awarded a penalty. Villa's goalkeeper kicked the ball out of the ground, and by the time the ball had been recovered, the 90minutes had elapsed and the game was over.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The same law also stands that the duration of either half is extended until the penalty kick to be taken or retaken is completed, thus no game shall end with a penalty to be taken.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1200" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg/220px-Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg/330px-Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg/440px-Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Didier_Drogba_Manuel_Neuer_last_penalty_kick_Champions_League_Final_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Some football competitions use a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penalty_shootout_(football)" title="Penalty shootout (football)">
         penalty shootout
        </a>
        to decide the winner if a match ends as a draw
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In league competitions, games may end in a draw. In knockout competitions where a winner is required various methods may be employed to break such a deadlock, some competitions may invoke
      <a href="/wiki/Replay_(sports)" title="Replay (sports)">
       replays
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A game tied at the end of regulation time may go into extra time, which consists of two further 15-minute periods. If the score is still tied after extra time, some competitions allow the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penalty_shootout_(football)" title="Penalty shootout (football)">
       penalty shootouts
      </a>
      (known officially in the Laws of the Game as "kicks from the penalty mark") to determine which team will progress to the next stage of the tournament. Goals scored during extra time periods count toward the final score of the game, but kicks from the penalty mark are only used to decide the team that progresses to the next part of the tournament (with goals scored in a penalty shootout not making up part of the final score).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laws51-52_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-laws51-52-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In competitions using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-legged_match" title="Two-legged match">
       two-legged matches
      </a>
      , each team competes at home once, with an aggregate score from the two matches deciding which team progresses. Where aggregates are equal, the
      <a href="/wiki/Away_goals_rule" title="Away goals rule">
       away goals rule
      </a>
      may be used to determine the winners, in which case the winner is the team that scored the most goals in the leg they played away from home. If the result is still equal, extra time and potentially a penalty shootout are required.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laws51-52_45-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-laws51-52-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the late 1990s and early 2000s, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IFAB" title="IFAB">
       IFAB
      </a>
      experimented with ways of creating a winner without requiring a penalty shootout, which was often seen as an undesirable way to end a match. These involved rules ending a game in extra time early, either when the first goal in extra time was scored (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal" title="Golden goal">
        golden goal
       </a>
      </i>
      ), or if one team held a lead at the end of the first period of extra time (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_goal" title="Silver goal">
        silver goal
       </a>
      </i>
      ). Golden goal was used at the World Cup in
      <a href="/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1998 FIFA World Cup">
       1998
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/2002_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2002 FIFA World Cup">
       2002
      </a>
      . The first World Cup game decided by a golden goal was
      <a href="/wiki/France_national_football_team" title="France national football team">
       France
      </a>
      's victory over
      <a href="/wiki/Paraguay_national_football_team" title="Paraguay national football team">
       Paraguay
      </a>
      in 1998.
      <a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">
       Germany
      </a>
      was the first nation to score a golden goal in a major competition, beating
      <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic_national_football_team" title="Czech Republic national football team">
       Czech Republic
      </a>
      in the final of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euro_1996" title="Euro 1996">
       Euro 1996
      </a>
      . Silver goal was used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euro_2004" title="Euro 2004">
       Euro 2004
      </a>
      . Both these experiments have been discontinued by IFAB.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ball_in_and_out_of_play">
       Ball in and out of play
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ball_in_and_out_of_play" title="Ball in and out of play">
       Ball in and out of play
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Under the Laws, the two basic states of play during a game are
      <i>
       ball in play
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       ball out of play
      </i>
      . From the beginning of each playing period with a kick-off until the end of the playing period, the ball is in play at all times, except when either the ball leaves the field of play, or play is stopped by the referee. When the ball becomes out of play, play is restarted by one of eight restart methods depending on how it went out of play:
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1856" data-file-width="2641" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg/220px-Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg/330px-Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg/440px-Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shunsuke1_20080622.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A player takes a free kick, while the opposition form a "wall" to try to block the ball
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Kick-off_(association_football)" title="Kick-off (association football)">
        Kick-off
       </a>
       : following a goal by the opposing team, or to begin each period of play.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restart_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-restart-49">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         49
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Throw-in" title="Throw-in">
        Throw-in
       </a>
       : when the ball has crossed the touchline; awarded to the opposing team to that which last touched the ball.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Goal_kick" title="Goal kick">
        Goal kick
       </a>
       : when the ball has wholly crossed the goal line without a goal having been scored and having last been touched by a player of the attacking team; awarded to defending team.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         77
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Corner_kick" title="Corner kick">
        Corner kick
       </a>
       : when the ball has wholly crossed the goal line without a goal having been scored and having last been touched by a player of the defending team; awarded to attacking team.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Indirect_free_kick" title="Indirect free kick">
        Indirect free kick
       </a>
       : awarded to the opposing team following "non-penal" fouls, certain technical infringements, or when play is stopped to caution or dismiss an opponent without a specific foul having occurred. A goal may not be scored directly (without the ball first touching another player) from an indirect free kick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freekick_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-freekick-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Direct_free_kick" title="Direct free kick">
        Direct free kick
       </a>
       : awarded to fouled team following certain listed "penal" fouls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freekick_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-freekick-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       A goal may be scored directly from a direct free kick.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Penalty_kick" title="Penalty kick">
        Penalty kick
       </a>
       : awarded to the fouled team following a foul usually punishable by a direct free kick but that has occurred within their opponent's penalty area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         80
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Dropped-ball" title="Dropped-ball">
        Dropped-ball
       </a>
       : occurs when the referee has stopped play for any other reason, such as a serious injury to a player, interference by an external party, or a ball becoming defective.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restart_49-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-restart-49">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         49
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Misconduct">
       Misconduct
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foul_(association_football)" title="Foul (association football)">
       Foul (association football)
      </a>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="On-field">
       On-field
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:128px;max-width:128px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_card.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="200" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Yellow_card.svg/60px-Yellow_card.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Yellow_card.svg/90px-Yellow_card.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Yellow_card.svg/120px-Yellow_card.svg.png 2x" width="60"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_card.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="200" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Red_card.svg/60px-Red_card.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Red_card.svg/90px-Red_card.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Red_card.svg/120px-Red_card.svg.png 2x" width="60"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
        Players are cautioned with a yellow card, and dismissed from the game with a red card. These colours were first introduced at the
        <a href="/wiki/1970_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1970 FIFA World Cup">
         1970 FIFA World Cup
        </a>
        and used consistently since.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg/220px-Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg/330px-Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg/440px-Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ryan_Valentine_scores.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A player scores a penalty kick given after an offence is committed inside the penalty area
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foul_(football)" title="Foul (football)">
       foul
      </a>
      occurs when a player commits an offence listed in the Laws of the Game while the ball is in play. The offences that constitute a foul are listed in Law 12. Handling the ball deliberately, tripping an opponent, or pushing an opponent, are examples of "penal fouls", punishable by a
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_free_kick" title="Direct free kick">
       direct free kick
      </a>
      or penalty kick depending on where the offence occurred. Other fouls are punishable by an
      <a href="/wiki/Indirect_free_kick" title="Indirect free kick">
       indirect free kick
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fouls_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fouls-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The referee may punish a player's or substitute's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Misconduct_(football)" title="Misconduct (football)">
